The Subject Matter of Ijarah: Contract on the Asset, Not the Benefit
Explains that ijarah contract is on the 'ayn as a substitute for the nonexistent benefit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that an ijarah contract is concluded on the 'ayn, explain why benefits cannot be the mahall, and distinguish between the definitions of the author and al-Quduri.

Core Idea
The ijarah contract is on the asset, not the benefit — because nonexistent cannot be a subject matter.

Ijarah, or leasing, appears straightforward: you pay for the use of something. But in Hanafi fiqh, the contract's subject matter (mahall) must exist at the time of the contract. Benefits are nonexistent at that moment — they materialize gradually. So how can a lease be valid? The classical solution is clever: the contract is actually on the physical asset itself, which stands in for the benefits. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and the subtle definitions of ijarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core problem
Understand that benefits (manafi') are nonexistent at contract time. They come into being moment by moment. According to Hanafi usul, the mahall of a contract must exist at the time of contracting for the contract to be valid. So benefits cannot be the direct subject matter.
This is a fundamental condition in Hanafi contract law: the mahall must be existent, specific, and deliverable.
2
Comprehend the Hanafi solution
To overcome this, the physical asset ('ayn) is made the mahall. The asset exists, so it satisfies the condition. The contract is thus concluded on the asset, which represents the benefits. This is why we say 'I lease you this house' rather than 'I lease you the benefits of this house'.
The asset acts as a proxy; the actual owned item is still the benefits, but the contract's subject matter is the asset.
3
Recognize the practical consequence
If someone mistakenly attaches the contract to the benefits directly, the lease is invalid (batil). For example, 'I lease you the benefits of this car for a month' is invalid; it must be 'I lease you this car for a month'. The benefits are what is owned (mamluk), but the mahall is the car.
This distinction protects the contract from gharar (uncertainty) and ensures the mahall is known and existent at the outset.
4
Differentiate between definitions
The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines ijarah as 'a sale of a known thing for a known wage' (focusing on the 'ayn as mahall). Al-Quduri defines it as 'a contract on benefits in exchange for compensation'. The author's definition is more precise because it implies the mahall is an existing thing (the 'ayn), while al-Quduri's directly mentions benefits, which are nonexistent. The text argues the author's definition is better.
This is a critical refinement: the Hanafi definition must safeguard the mahall condition. Al-Quduri's wording could be misleading if taken literally.
5
Grasp the technical terms: 'illah and hukm delay
The contract itself is called the 'illah (cause) that produces ownership of benefits. The ownership (hukm) does not arise instantly but rather appears moment by moment as benefits emerge. This is compared to a sale with a right of option where ownership is delayed. The expression 'concluded moment by moment' refers to the continuous effectiveness of the cause, not a renewal of offer and acceptance each moment.
This technical analysis shows the sophistication of Hanafi legal reasoning: the 'illah is operative over time, and the hukm can be delayed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف رحمه الله تعالى إلى أن عقد الإجارة ينعقد بإقامة العين مقام المنفعة حق الانعقاد لا في حق الملك لأن العقد لا بد له من محل لأنه شرط للصحة لقول الفقهاء المحال شروط ومحل العقد هنا المنافع وهي معدومة والمعدوم لا يصلح محلاً فجعلت الدار محلاً بإقامتها مقام المنافع، ولهذا لو أضاف العقد إلى المنافع لا يجوز بأن قال أجرتك منافع هذه الدار شهراً بكذا، وإنما يصح بإضافته إلى العين والمراد من انعقاد العلة ساعة فساعة في كلام مشايخنا على حسب حدوث المنافع هو عمل العلة ونفاذها في المحل ساعة فساعة لا ارتباط الإيجاب والقبول كل ساعة وإن كان ظاهر كلام مشايخنا يوهم ذلك. والحكم تأخر من زمان انعقاد العلة إلى حدوث المنافع ساعة فساعة لأن الحكم قابل للتراخي كما في البيع بشرط الخيار، ثم عقد الإجارة على ما عرف في أصول الفقه علة اسماً لإضافة الحكم إليه ومعنى لكونه مؤثراً لا حكماً لتراخي الحكم عنه ؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وبهذا تبين أن تعريف المصنف أولى من تعريف القدوري بقوله عقد على المنافع بعوض» لما علمت أنها عقد على العين وإنما المملوك المنافع.
And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated that the ijarah contract is concluded by making the physical asset stand in place of the benefit, with respect to the conclusion not with respect to ownership, because the contract must have a subject matter (mahall) for it is a condition of validity; the jurists say 'the subject matters are conditions.' The subject matter here is the benefits, and they are nonexistent, and the nonexistent cannot be a subject matter. So the house is made a subject matter by making it stand in place of the benefits. For this reason, if one attaches the contract to the benefits, it is not permissible, like saying 'I lease to you the benefits of this house for a month for such.' Rather, it is valid only by attaching it to the physical object. And the meaning of 'the cause (illah) being concluded moment by moment' in the speech of our teachers is, according to the occurrence of the benefits, the action of the cause and its effectiveness in the subject matter moment by moment, not the connection of offer and acceptance each moment, although the apparent meaning of our teachers' speech may suggest that. And the ruling is delayed from the time of the cause's conclusion to the occurrence of the benefits moment by moment, because the ruling can be delayed, as in a sale with a condition of option. Then the ijarah contract, as known in usul al-fiqh, is a cause in name (adding the ruling to it) and in meaning (being effectual), not a ruling because the ruling is delayed from it. Likewise in Ghayat al-Bayan. And by this it becomes clear that the author's definition is better than al-Quduri's definition 'a contract on benefits in exchange for compensation', because you know that it is a contract on the 'ayn, and what is owned is the benefits.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage provides a sophisticated analysis of the subject matter (mahall) of the ijarah contract. Since benefits are nonexistent at the time of contract (they come into being gradually), they cannot serve as the direct subject matter. Therefore, the contract is technically concluded on the physical asset (e.g., the house), which acts as a proxy for the benefits. This is why it is invalid to say 'I lease the benefits of this house' but valid to say 'I lease this house.' The passage also clarifies the meaning of 'concluded moment by moment' in the works of our scholars: it refers to the continuous effectiveness of the cause (the contract) in generating ownership of the benefits as they arise, not to a renewal of offer and acceptance. The distinction between the author's definition and al-Quduri's is that the author defines ijarah as a sale of a known thing for a known wage (focusing on the 'ayn), while al-Quduri defines it as a contract on benefits. The author's definition is preferred because the contract is on the 'ayn, but the owned object is the benefit.

Key Terms
محل العقد (mahall al-'aqd): The subject matter of the contract, which must be existing at the time of contract; in ijarah the 'ayn substitutes for the nonexistent benefit.
العلة (al-'illah): The cause (the contract itself) that produces the ruling (ownership of benefits) progressively.
الحكم (al-hukm): The legal effect (ownership of benefits) that arises after the contract, not instantly but as benefits appear.
المملوك (al-mamluk): The owned object; in ijarah, the benefits are owned even though the contract is on the 'ayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because benefits are nonexistent and cannot be a subject matter
B) Because the Arabic grammar is incorrect
C) Because benefits are owned by the lessor
D) Because the house is not mentioned

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, the contract is concluded on the physical asset ('ayn) as a proxy for the nonexistent benefits; the owned object is the benefits.

Intended Benefit as a Condition for Valid Ijarah
States ruling with proof and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of maqsuda benefit to identify when an ijarah contract is fasid and state the ruling that no wage is due.

Core Idea
Ijarah is valid only if the benefit is customarily intended from the asset; unusual uses like display void the contract.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of an ijarah contract depends not only on the existence of a benefit but on its nature. The benefit must be one that is ordinarily sought from the particular object. This lesson unpacks that condition using classical examples and explains why such contracts are fasid (voidable) rather than batil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
The passage begins by stating that the relevant benefit is 'al-manfa'ah al-maqsudah' (the intended benefit). Understand that this is a condition for ijarah to be valid.
The text uses 'fasidah' for all examples – the contract is invalid due to a deficiency, not void ab initio.
2
Review the examples of invalid ijarah
Six examples are given: hiring garments to spread without sitting, a donkey to tie for show, a spare animal (jannibah), vessels for adornment, a house to appear owned, a slave not to be employed, and dirhams to display. For each, the intended use is not the customary benefit of the asset.
In each case, the hirer does not intend to use the object for its normal purpose.
3
Understand the legal consequence
The passage rules that in all these cases, the ijarah is fasid and the hirer has no right to wage (no ajr). The contract is not completely void but is defective, so the wage is not obligatory.
Fasid means the contract is invalid due to a missing condition; it can be rectified if the condition is met, but here it cannot.
4
Analyze the rationale (ta'lil)
Al-Bazzazi explains that the benefit in these cases is ghayr maqsuda (not intended). This is the underlying cause (illah) for invalidity. The benefit must be one that the law and custom consider as the purpose of leasing that asset.
This is a talil (causative reasoning) – the rule is based on a clear rationale.
5
Contrast with 'ariyah
The text notes that in 'ariyah (loan for gratuitous use), borrowing dirhams for adornment is valid as a loan, not as ijarah. This shows that the condition of maqsuda benefit applies specifically to compensatory contracts like ijarah, not to gratuitous ones.
The example from Kitab al-'Ariyah in al-Khulasa highlights the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد من المنفعة المنفعة المقصودة من العين حتى لو استأجر ثياباً ليبسطها ولا يقعد عليها ولا ينام أو دابة ليربطها في فنائه ويظن الناس أنها له أو ليجعلها جنيبة بين يديه أو آنية يضعها في بيته يتجمل بها ولا يستعملها أو داراً لا يسكنها لكن ليظن الناس أنها له ملكاً أو عبداً على أن لا يستخدمه أو دراهم يضعها فالإجارة في جميع فاسدة ولا أجرة له؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وعلل البزازي في فتاواه بأنها منفعة غير مقصودة من العين، وذكر في الخلاصة في كتاب العارية أنه لو استعار دارهم ليتجمل بها كانت عارية لا قرضاً ا فأفاد أن العارية تخالف الإجارة في اشتراط ذلك اهـ.
And what is meant by benefit is the benefit intended (maqsuda) from the physical object. Such that if someone hires garments to spread them without sitting or sleeping on them, or a donkey to tie it in his courtyard and people think it is his, or to make it a jannibah (spare animal) beside him, or vessels to place in his house for adornment without using them, or a house not to live in but so that people think it is his property, or a slave on condition that he does not employ him, or dirhams to place them, then the ijarah in all of these is invalid (fasidah) and he has no wage. Likewise in al-Khulasa. And al-Bazzazi explained in his Fatawa that it is a benefit not intended from the asset. And it is mentioned in al-Khulasa in Kitab al-'Ariyah that if someone borrows dirhams for adornment, it is 'ariyah not qard; this shows that 'ariyah differs from ijarah in requiring that (i.e., the intended benefit).
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage, drawn from al-Khulasa, establishes that for an ijarah to be valid, the benefit must be the one commonly and lawfully intended from the asset. If the contract is for a benefit that is not genuinely sought from that type of property, the contract is fasid (invalid/voidable) and no wage is due. Examples include hiring clothes merely to spread (not to wear or sit on), a donkey to tie as a show of ownership, a house for showing off ownership rather than residing, a slave with a stipulation not to use him, or dirhams just to display. Al-Bazzazi explains the rationale: the benefit is not maqsuda (intended) from such assets. The text contrasts with 'ariyah (loan for use), where such unusual uses may be valid as a loan but not as ijarah because the conditions differ.

Key Terms
المنفعة المقصودة (al-manfa'ah al-maqsūdah): The benefit that is customarily and legally intended from an asset, such as riding from a horse or sitting from a garment.
جنيبة (jannībah): A spare animal led alongside without riding, used for show or backup.
عارية ('āriyah): A loan of an object for gratuitous use, without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Hiring a horse for riding
D) Hiring a room for storage of goods

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, the benefit must be the one customarily intended from the asset; otherwise, the contract is fasid and no wage is due.

Valid Price is Valid Wage: Conditions for Different Types of Wages
Establishes the principle that valid price in sale is valid wage in ijarah, with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule 'what is valid as a price is valid as a wage' to determine the validity of wages in ijarah, distinguishing between currency, measured/weighed items, goods ('urud), and animals, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference regarding place of delivery.

Core Idea
Valid price = valid wage, but benefits can be wages for benefits of different types.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq elaborates the foundational maxim of ijarah: whatever is valid as a price (thaman) in a sale is valid as a wage (ujrah) in ijarah. However, the author carefully distinguishes between different types of wages and specifies the conditions each must fulfill, drawing on the rules of salam and qard. A key intra-Hanafi difference emerges regarding the specification of place of delivery for measured/weighed items. Understanding these nuances is crucial for drafting valid ijarah contracts.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule and its basis
The rule: 'What is valid as a price is valid as a wage.' The basis: the wage is the price of the usufruct, so it follows the rules of price in sale.
This is a general rule of thumb, but has exceptions.
2
Identify the types of wages covered
The passage discusses: currency (dirhams/dinars), measured/weighed/counted items, goods ('urud), animals, and benefits as wages.
Each type has its own conditions.
3
Apply conditions for each type
For currency: refer to the prevalent local currency; if mixed, specify. For measured/weighed/counted: specify quantity and quality; and per Abu Hanifa, also place of delivery if transport cost. For 'urud: must meet salam conditions (quantity, term, quality). For animals: must be specifically identified. For benefits: valid only if different types.
Salam conditions apply to 'urud because they only become a debt via salam.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on place of delivery
Abu Hanifa requires specification of place of delivery for measured/weighed items with transport cost; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not.
The mufta bihi position is likely that of the companions for leniency.
5
Understand the exception to the reverse rule
The rule does not reverse: benefits of different types can be wages even though they cannot be prices. For example, leasing a house in exchange for cultivation of land is valid.
Same-genre benefits are not allowed as wages for each other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وما صح ثمناً صح أجرة) أي ما جاز أن يكون ثمناً في البيع جاز أن يكون أجرة في الإجارة لأن الأجرة ثمن المنفعة فتعتبر بثمن المبيع. ومراده من الثمن ما كان بدلاً عن شيء فدخل فيه الأعيان فإن العين تصلح بدلاً في المقايضة فتصلح أجرة. وأشار المصنف إلى أنها لو كانت الأجرة دراهم أو دنانير انصرفت إلى غالب نقد البلد، فإن كانت الغلبة مختلفة فالإجارة فاسدة ما لم يبين نقداً منها، فإن بين جاز فإنها لو كانت كيلياً أو وزنياً أو عددياً متقارباً فالشرط فيه بيان القدر والصفة ويحتاج فيه إلى بيان مكان الإيفاء إذا كان له حمل ومؤنة عند أبي حنيفة، وإن لم يكن له حمل ومؤنة فلا يحتاج إلى بيان مكان الإيفاء. وعندهما ليس بشرط ولا يحتاج إلى بيان الأجل، فإن بين جاز وثبت وأنها لو كانت ثياباً أو عروضاً فالشرط فيه بيان القدر والأجل والصفة لأنه لا يثبت ديناً في الذمة إلا من جهة السلم فكان لثبوته أصل واحد وهو السلم فلا يجوز إلا على شرائط السلم بخلاف الكيلي والوزني لأن لثبوتهما أصلين : القرض والسلم والأجل في القرض ليس بشرط فإن بين جاز كالسلم وإن لم يبين جاز كالقرض. وهذا كله إذا لم يشر إليها فإن أشار فهي كافية ولا يحتاج إلى بيان القدر والوصف والأجل، وأنها لو كانت حيواناً فلا يجوز إلا أن يكون معيناً؛ كذا ذكر الأسبيجابي في شرح مختصر الطحاوي. وأشار أيضاً إلى أن هذا الضابط لا ينعكس كلياً فلا يقال ما لا يجوز ثمناً لا يجوز أجرة لأن المنفعة يجوز أن تكون أجرة للمنفعة إذا كانت مختلفة الجنس كاستئجار سكنى الدار بزراعة الأرض وإن اتحد جنسهما لا يجوز كاستئجار الدار للسكنى بالسكني، وكاستئجار الأرض للزراعة بزراعة أرض أخرى لأن الجنس بانفراده يحرم قوله : (والمنفعة تعلم ببيان المدة كالسكنى والزراعة فتصح على مدة معلومة أي مدة كانت) لأن للنساء الأعيان قصداً لا تنعقد فتأمل . قوله: (وعندهما ليس بشرط) قال الرملي : وقدم في السلم أنه يتعين عندهما مكان الدار ومكان تسليم الدابة وفي الجوهرة: وعندهما لا يشترط ويسلمه عند الأرض المستأجرة قوله : (ولا يحتاج إلى بيان الأجل فإن بين جاز وثبت) قال الرملي قال في الجوهرة ولا يحتاج إلى بيان الأجل فإن الأجر صار مؤجلاً كالثمن في البيع اهـ. يعني ببيان المدة كما لو قال بعتك بكذا إلى شهر مثلاً تأمل .
His statement: (And what is valid as a price is valid as a wage), meaning whatever is permissible to be a price in a sale is permissible to be a wage in ijarah, because the wage is the price of the usufruct, so it is considered like the price of the sold item. And by 'price' he means whatever is a substitute for something, so physical objects are included, for an object is valid as a substitute in barter, so it is valid as a wage. And the author indicated that if the wage is dirhams or dinars, they are referred to the prevalent currency of the land. If the prevalence is differing, then the ijarah is invalid unless a specific currency is stated; if stated, it is valid. If the wage is a measured (kayli), weighed (wazni), or closely counted (adadi mutaqarib) item, then it is necessary to specify the quantity and quality, and according to Abu Hanifa, also needed is specification of the place of delivery if it has transport cost; if it has no transport cost, then no need to specify place. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not a condition and there is no need to specify the term (ajal). If mentioned, it is valid and established. And if the wage is cloths or goods (urud), then it is necessary to specify quantity, term, and quality, because it does not become a debt in the dhimmah except by way of salam. So it has one basis for being established, salam, so it is only valid on the conditions of salam. Unlike measured and weighed items, because they have two bases: qard and salam, and the term is not a condition in qard, so if the term is mentioned it is like salam, if not mentioned it is like qard. All this if the wage is not pointed to; if it is pointed to, then that suffices and there is no need to specify quantity, description, or term. And if the wage is an animal, it is not valid unless it is specifically identified. So mentioned al-Asbijabi in his commentary on Mukhtasar al-Tahawi. And he also indicated that this rule does not apply completely in reverse; one cannot say 'what is not valid as a price is not valid as a wage', because a benefit can be a wage for a benefit if they are of different types, like leasing a house for cultivation of land, but if they are of the same type, it is not valid, like leasing a house to dwell in for another house, or leasing land for cultivation with cultivation of another land, because a single type alone is prohibited. His statement: (And the benefit is known by specifying the duration, like residence and cultivation, so it is valid for a known duration, any duration) because ... (for women?) the physical objects are intended, not concluded, so ponder. His statement: (And according to them it is not a condition) al-Ramli said: And he presented in al-Salam that according to them the place of the house and the place of delivery of the animal are determined, and in al-Jawharah: And according to them it is not stipulated and he delivers it at the rented land. His statement: (And there is no need to specify the term; if specified, it is valid and established) al-Ramli said, quoting al-Jawharah: There is no need to specify the term, because the wage becomes deferred like the price in a sale. Meaning by specifying the duration, like if he said 'I sell to you for such, to a month', for example. Ponder.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This lesson covers the extensive commentary on the maxim 'What is valid as a price (thaman) is valid as a wage (ujrah).' The author clarifies that the wage is the price of the usufruct, so the same rules apply. However, the reverse is not always true: a benefit can be a wage for another benefit. The passage then details the requirements for various types of wages: currency (dirhams/dinars) follows local convention; measured/weighed items need specification of quantity and quality, and per Abu Hanifa also place of delivery if there is transport cost; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not require place specification. For goods ('urud) like cloth, the conditions of salam apply (since debt is only established by salam for these items). If the wage item is physically pointed to, then no detailed description is needed. Animals must be specifically identified. The passage also discusses the term (ajal) and its necessity. Finally, a useful distinction is made between same-genre and different-genre exchanges of benefits. The marginal commentary from al-Ramli and al-Jawharah further clarifies the positions of the two companions.

Key Terms
الثمن (al-thaman): The price in a sale; anything valid as thaman is valid as ujrah (wage).
العروض (al-'urūḍ): Goods other than currency, measured/weighed items, and animals; includes cloth etc.
السلم (al-salam): A sale with advanced payment and deferred delivery of specified goods; its conditions govern when 'urud are given as wage.
الحمل والمؤنة (al-haml wa al-mu'nah): Transport cost; if the wage item has transport cost, Abu Hanifa requires place of delivery specification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is required for a wage paid in goods ('urud) like cloth?
A) Only quantity and quality
B) Quantity, quality, and term
C) Only the item must be pointed to
D) Quantity, quality, term, and place of delivery

Key Takeaway
The rule 'what is valid as a price is valid as a wage' is a general principle, but each type of wage has its own conditions that must be met for the ijarah to be valid.

The Wage Must Be Known for Valid Ijarah
Condition and examples of unknown wage in ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that an ijarah contract requires a known wage and apply this condition to determine contract validity.

Core Idea
A wage unknown is a contract undone — uncertainty in the essential term invalidates.

In an ijarah (hire/lease) contract, the wage (ujrah) is a fundamental element. Its specification is a condition for validity. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that if the wage is unknown (majhul), the contract is irregular (fasid). It provides examples and a dispute resolution rule to illustrate the requirement of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The author states that the wage must be known (ma'lum). This is a shart (condition) for the contract's validity. If unknown, the contract is not valid.
The condition applies to the wage itself, not the work.
2
Apply to examples
Consider the examples: hiring a slave 'with his food' or an animal 'with its fodder' as wage. The food/fodder is uncertain in value, so the wage is unknown, making the contract invalid (la yajuz).
The uncertainty (jahalah) arises from the lack of specification.
3
Note the exception
The wet nurse (zhi'r) is excepted from this rule, as will be explained later. This indicates that custom or necessity may relax the condition in some cases.
The exception is not detailed here; it serves as a preview.
4
Handle the dispute scenario
If the parties disagree on the wage amount and separate without agreement, the owner's claim (twenty) prevails unless the hirer accepts (ten). This shows that the wage must be definite at contract formation.
The ruling applies when no agreement is reached; if they agreed, that would be binding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحترز بقوله بأجر معلوم عما إذا كان مجهولاً كما إذا استأجر عبداً بأجر معلوم وبطعامه لا يجوز، وكذا لو استأجر دابة بعلفها لا يجوز للجهالة بخلاف الظئر كما سيأتي؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وفيها أيضاً : رجل استأجر من آخر غلاماً فقال صاحب الغلام بعشرين وقال المستأجر بعشرة فافترقوا على ذلك قال هو بعشرين إلا أن يرضي الذي أجره بعشرة .
And he [the author] made an exception with his statement 'with a known wage' from the case where it is unknown, such as if one hires a slave with a known wage and with his food, it is not valid. Likewise, if one hires an animal with its fodder, it is not valid due to ignorance [of the wage], unlike the wet nurse, as will come. This is in al-Khulasa. And in it also: A man hired a boy from another. The owner of the boy said [the wage is] twenty, and the hirer said ten, and they parted on that. He said: It is twenty, unless the one who hired is satisfied with ten.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the condition that the wage (ujrah) must be known (ma'lum) in an ijarah contract. The author first mentions that the wage being known is a condition, and ignorance (jahl) of the wage invalidates the contract. He gives examples: hiring a slave with his food as part of the wage is invalid because the value of the food is unknown. Similarly, hiring an animal with its fodder is invalid. An exception is the wet nurse (zhi'r) as will be explained later. Then he relates a case from al-Khulasa: if there is a dispute about the wage amount—the owner claims twenty, the hirer claims ten—and they separate without agreement, the wage is set at twenty unless the owner accepts ten. This shows that the wage must be definite.

Key Terms
أجر معلوم (ajrun ma'lum): The wage that is fixed and known to both parties in an ijarah contract.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty about an essential element of the contract, leading to invalidity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a person hires an animal with its fodder as the wage?
A) Valid, because fodder is a common form of payment.
B) Invalid, because the value of fodder is unknown (jahalah).
C) Valid, if the animal is used for plowing.
D) Invalid only if the fodder is not specified by type.

Key Takeaway
For an ijarah contract to be valid under Hanafi fiqh, the wage must be known (ma'lum); ignorance of the wage renders the contract fasid.

The Rule of Thaman and Ujrah: Conditions and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Place of Delivery
Elucidation of the rule that thaman (price) and ujrah (wage) share the same conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the general fiqh rule that what is valid as a price is valid as a wage, and distinguish the conditions for different types of wages, including the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding place of delivery for kayli/wazni items.

Core Idea
A valid price is a valid wage, but conditions differ by wage type and by imam.

In Hanafi fiqh, the wage (ujrah) in an ijarah contract is treated analogously to the price (thaman) in a sale. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq elaborates on this principle, detailing the specific conditions required for wages depending on their type, such as dirhams, weighed items, or goods. It also highlights an important intra-Hanafi difference: Imam Abu Hanifa requires specifying the place of delivery for certain wages if transport cost is involved, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not. Understanding these nuances is essential for drafting valid ijarah contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recognize that anything valid as a price (thaman) is also valid as a wage (ujrah) because the wage is the price of the benefit. The term 'thaman' includes both coins and physical objects.
2
Categorize wage types
The text classifies wages into dirhams/dinars, kayli/wazni/adadi mutaqarrib, garments/goods (urud), and animals. Each has distinct conditions.
3
Analyze conditions for each type
Dirhams/dinars: if not specified, refer to prevalent coinage; if coinage differs, contract invalid unless specified. Kayli/wazni: quantity and quality required; place of delivery required by Abu Hanifa if transport cost, not by the two companions. Garments/goods: conditions of salam (quantity, quality, term) because only established as debt via salam. If item is physically pointed to, conditions relax.
4
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa requires specifying place of delivery for kayli/wazni if transport and expense; the two companions say it is not a condition. This affects validity: if place not specified, contract is fasid according to Abu Hanifa, sahih according to the two.
5
Apply the rule to animals and specified items
Animals must be specifically identified; pointing to an item suffices and removes need for detailed specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وما صح ثمناً صح أجرة) أي ما جاز أن يكون ثمناً في البيع جاز أن يكون أجرة في الإجارة لأن الأجرة ثمن المنفعة فتعتبر بثمن المبيع. ومراده من الثمن ما كان بدلاً عن شيء فدخل فيه الأعيان فإن العين تصلح بدلاً في المقايضة فتصلح أجرة. وأشار المصنف إلى أنها لو كانت الأجرة دراهم أو دنانير انصرفت إلى غالب نقد البلد، فإن كانت الغلبة مختلفة فالإجارة فاسدة ما لم يبين نقداً منها، فإن بين جاز فإنها لو كانت كيلياً أو وزنياً أو عددياً متقارباً فالشرط فيه بيان القدر والصفة ويحتاج فيه إلى بيان مكان الإيفاء إذا كان له حمل ومؤنة عند أبي حنيفة، وإن لم يكن له حمل ومؤنة فلا يحتاج إلى بيان مكان الإيفاء. وعندهما ليس بشرط ولا يحتاج إلى بيان الأجل، فإن بين جاز وثبت وأنها لو كانت ثياباً أو عروضاً فالشرط فيه بيان القدر والأجل والصفة لأنه لا يثبت ديناً في الذمة إلا من جهة السلم فكان لثبوته أصل واحد وهو السلم فلا يجوز إلا على شرائط السلم بخلاف الكيلي والوزني لأن لثبوتهما أصلين : القرض والسلم والأجل في القرض ليس بشرط فإن بين جاز كالسلم وإن لم يبين جاز كالقرض. وهذا كله إذا لم يشر إليها فإن أشار فهي كافية ولا يحتاج إلى بيان القدر والوصف والأجل، وأنها لو كانت حيواناً فلا يجوز إلا أن يكون معيناً؛ كذا ذكر الأسبيجابي في شرح مختصر الطحاوي.
His statement: 'And whatever is valid as a price is valid as a wage.' That is, whatever is permitted to be a price in sale is permitted to be a wage in ijarah, because the wage is the price of the benefit, so it is considered in analogy with the price of the sold item. He intends by 'price' that which is a substitute for something, so it includes physical objects (a'yan), for a physical object can be a substitute in barter, so it can be a wage. And the author indicated that if the wage is dirhams or dinars, it refers to the prevalent coinage of the country. If the prevalence differs, then the ijarah is invalid unless a specific coinage is specified; if specified, it is valid. And if the wage is a weighed (kayli) or measured (wazni) item, or a counted item with approximate equivalence (adadi mutaqarrib), then the condition is specifying the quantity and quality, and it requires specifying the place of delivery if there is transport and expense according to Abu Hanifa. If there is no transport and expense, then it does not require specifying the place of delivery. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not a condition. And it does not require specifying the term (ajal); if specified, it is valid and established. And if the wage is garments or goods (urud), then the condition is specifying the quantity, term, and quality, because a debt in the liability (dhimmah) is only established through salam. So its establishment has one origin, which is salam, so it is not valid except on the conditions of salam, unlike the weighed and measured items because their establishment has two origins: loan (qard) and salam, and in loan the term is not a condition. So if specified, it is valid like salam; if not specified, it is valid like qard. All this is if it is not pointed to (mushar ilayhi). If it is pointed to, then that is sufficient and does not require specifying quantity, description, and term. And if the wage is an animal, it is not valid unless it is specifically identified. Thus mentioned by al-Asbajabi in his commentary on Mukhtasar al-Tahawi.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the rule that anything valid as a price (thaman) in a sale is also valid as a wage (ujrah) in an ijarah contract, because the wage is the price of the benefit. The commentator explains that the term 'thaman' here includes physical objects, which can be used in barter. He then details the conditions for different types of wage. For dirhams and dinars, if not specified, it refers to the prevalent coinage; if the prevalence differs, the contract is invalid unless specified. For measured or weighed items, the quantity and quality must be specified, and according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the place of delivery must also be specified if transport costs are involved; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree and say it is not a condition. The term (ajal) is not required for these items. For garments or goods, the conditions of salam apply because they are not established as debt except through salam, so quantity, quality, and term are required. However, if the item is physically pointed to, then that suffices. Finally, animals must be specifically identified to be valid as wage. This shows the detailed rules ensuring certainty in the contract.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale, considered as a substitute (badal) for the sold item.
أجرة (ujrah): The wage in an ijarah contract, considered as the price (thaman) of the benefit.
السلم (al-salam): A sale where the price is paid in advance and the goods are delivered later; used as a standard for deferred obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is required when the wage is a weighed item (wazni) according to Abu Hanifa if it involves transport cost?
A) Only quantity and quality
B) Quantity, quality, and place of delivery
C) Quantity, quality, place, and term
D) Only specification of the item by pointing

Key Takeaway
Anything valid as a price is valid as a wage, but additional conditions apply depending on the wage's type, and an intra-Hanafi difference exists regarding place of delivery for certain items.

Benefit in Ijarah is Known by Specifying Duration or Work
Explanation of how the benefit (manfa'ah) is made known in ijarah contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for making the benefit known in an ijarah contract and apply the ruling to scenarios with specified durations, including unusually long ones and future-start leases.

Core Idea
In ijarah, the benefit is known by specifying duration; any known duration, even beyond normal life, is valid.

This passage clarifies a key requirement in ijarah contracts: the benefit (manfa'ah) must be known to avoid uncertainty (gharar). The commentator explains that specifying the duration is sufficient, and that the lease is valid even if the duration seems unrealistically long. Understanding this rule helps avoid invalid leases and clarifies the effect of selling the property before the lease begins.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal ruling
The ijarah contract is valid (sahih) if the duration is known. This is the core hukm stated in the matn.
2
Determine the condition for benefit knowledge
The benefit is made known by specifying the duration (or describing the work). The passage gives examples of residence and cultivation.
3
Apply the intra-Hanafi dispute on duration length
Note that some scholars consider it invalid if the duration is excessively long, but the chosen view (mufta bihi) follows al-Khassaf in permitting any known duration.
Al-Khassaf's view is that knowledge of duration suffices regardless of whether the lessee lives that long.
4
Recognize the effect of adding the lease to a future time (mudaf)
The lease can be attached to a future time (e.g., 'starting tomorrow'). The lessor retains the right to sell the property before the lease starts, and such sale rescinds the lease.
This is from al-Khulasa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والمنفعة تعلم ببيان المدة كالسكنى والزراعة فتصح على مدة معلومة أي مدة كانت) لأن المدة إذا كانت معلومة كان قدر المنفعة فيها معلوماً فأفاد أنها تجوز ولو كانت المدة لا يعيش إلى مثلها عادة، واختاره الخصاف ومنعه بعضهم. وأفاد أنها تجوز مضافاً كما لو قال أجرتك هذه الدار غداً وللمؤجر بيعها اليوم وتنتقض الإجارة كما في الخلاصة.
His statement: 'And the benefit is known by specifying the duration, like residence and cultivation, so it is valid for a known duration, any duration it may be' because if the duration is known, the amount of benefit is known. So it indicates that it is valid even if the duration is such that one would not normally live that long, and al-Khassaf chose this, and some prohibited it. And it indicates that it is valid when added (mudaf) to the future, as if he says: I lease this house to you tomorrow, and the lessor may sell it today, and the lease is rescinded, as in al-Khulasa.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage explains how the benefit (manfa'ah) in an ijarah contract is made known. The commentator expands on the matn statement. The benefit can be known by specifying the duration, like for residence or cultivation. Any specific duration is valid, even if the hireling is not expected to live that long, according to al-Khassaf, though some scholars prohibit it. Additionally, it is valid to add the lease to a future time (e.g., 'I lease you this house starting tomorrow'). In that case, the lessor can still sell the house today, and the lease is then rescinded upon sale, as mentioned in al-Khulasa.

Key Terms
منفعة (manfa'ah): The benefit or usufruct that is the subject of the ijarah contract.
مضاف (mudaf): A contract that is added to a future time, e.g., 'I lease this to you tomorrow' rather than immediately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, is it valid to lease a house for a duration that exceeds the normal lifespan of the lessee?
A) No, it is invalid
B) Yes, according to al-Khassaf
C) Only if the lessee is young
D) Only for waqf property

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, the benefit is known by specifying a duration, and any such duration, even if unreasonably long, suffices for validity.

Duration and Validity of Long Leases for Waqf and Orphan Property
Detailed ruling on long lease duration and contract unity dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the maximum duration of a waqf lease and explain the intra-madhhab dispute over whether a long lease is one contract or multiple.

Core Idea
A long lease with skewed rent is invalid; the dispute is whether partial invalidity cancels the whole contract.

This lesson examines the rules for leasing waqf and orphan property, focusing on duration. The source begins with Fatawa Qadikhan addressing a 'formal long lease' where rent is concentrated in the last year. Such a lease is invalid for the first years because the rent is below market value. This leads to a key intra-Hanafi dispute: is the multi-year lease one contract or several? The answer determines whether the beneficial part can be salvaged.

Guided Path
1
Identify the lease structure
Recognize the 'formal long lease' (ijarah tawilah rasmiah): a multi-year lease where rent is heavily weighted toward the final year, often with minimal rent in early years. This creates unequal compensation.
This structure is common in some historical contexts to disguise interest-like returns.
2
Understand the two opinions on contract unity
Some scholars view the multi-year lease as a single contract; others see it as separate annual contracts implicitly renewed. The text presents both views.
Those who see multiple contracts rely on the fact that each year's rent could be considered a separate lease.
3
Apply the consequence to validity
If a single contract, partial invalidity (due to below-market rent in early years) renders the entire lease invalid. If multiple contracts, only the invalid portions are void; valid parts (e.g., the last year if above market) stand.
The thamarah al-khilaf: the practical result differs significantly.
4
Determine the tarjih
The text states 'al-zahir huwa al-fasad fi al-kull' — the apparent view is total invalidity. This is the relied-upon position.
Thus, the entire long lease is invalid if any part fails market rent condition.
5
Know the duration limits
For waqf, the default maximum is three years (including lands). Sadr al-Shahid differentiated: three years for agricultural land, one year for other properties, unless maslahah necessitates longer with judge's permission.
This limit prevents tenants from claiming ownership through long possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : الوصي إذا أجر أرض اليتيم أو استأجر لليتيم أرضاً بمال اليتيم إجارة طويلة رسمية ثلاث سنين لا يجوز ذلك، وكذلك أبو الصغير ومتولي الوقف لأن الرسم في الإجارة الطويلة أن يجعل شيء يسير من مال الإجارة بمقابلة السنين الأول ومعظم المال بمقابلة السنة الأخيرة، فإن كانت الإجارة لأرض اليتيم أو الوقف لا تصح الإجارة في السنين الأول لأنها تكون بأقل من أجر المثل فلا تصح فإن استأجر أرضاً لليتيم أو الوقف بمال الوقف ففي السنة الأخيرة يكون الاستئجار بأكثر من أجر المثل فلا يصح، فإذا فسدت الإجارة في البعض في الوجهين هل يصح فيما كان خيراً لليتيم والوقف على قول من يجعل الإجارة الطويلة عقداً واحداً لا وعلى قول من يجعلها عقوداً يصح فيما كان خيراً لليتيم، ولا يصح فيما كان شراً له والظاهر هو الفساد في الكل ا هـ. قوله: (ولا تزاد في الأوقاف على ثلاث سنين كيلا يدعي المستأجر ملكها. قال في المختار . أطلقه فشمل الضياع وغيره. وقد أفتى الصدر الشهيد بعدم الزيادة على ثلاث سنين في الضياع وعلى سنة في غيرها إلا إذا كانت المصلحة في غيره. قال في المحيط : المختار للفتوى ا.هـ. ومراد المصنف عند عدم شرط الواقف فإن نص على شيء فاجره الناظر أكثر منه لا يجوز إلا إذا كانت إجارتها أكثر أنفع للفقراء والناس لا يرغبون في استئجارها فللقيم أن يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي حتى يؤاجرها أكثر لأن للقاضي ولاية النظر على الفقراء وعلى الميت أيضاً، وليس للقيم أن يؤاجرها بنفسه ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان. والمراد بعدم الجواز عدم الصحة يعني لو أجر الناظر الوقف أكثر من ثلاث سنين لا تصح الإجارة صرح به صدر الشريعة، وقيل تصح وتنفسخ ذكره الشمني واعلم أن إجارة الوقف لا تجوز إلا بأجرة المثل أو أكثر فلو أجر الناظر بدون أجرة المثل لا تصح الإجارة ويلزم المستأجر تمام أجر المثل، وقد وقع في الخلاصة عبارة أو همت أن الناظر يضمن تمام أجر المثل فقال : متولي الوقف أجر بدون أجر المثل يلزمه تمام أجر المثل ا هـ . وقد رده الشيخ قاسم في فتاواه بأن الضمير يرجع إلى المستأجر يدل عليه ما ذكره في تلخيص الفتاوى الكبرى وعبارته : كما قول المصنف : (ولا) تزاد في الأوقاف على ثلاث سنين قال الرملي وفي الجوهرة: وعلى هذا أرض اليتيم وقد أفتى صاحب البحر بإلحاق عقار اليتيم بالوقف وكذا تلميذه الشيخ العلامة الغزي، وأكثر كلامهم في المسألة يدل على أنه المختار وأنه المفتى به وعلته أنه كما يصان الوقف يصان مال
And in Fatawa Qadikhan: If the executor (wasi) leases the orphan's land or leases land for the orphan with the orphan's money in a long formal lease of three years, that is not valid. Likewise, the father of a minor (abu al-saghir) and the trustee of a waqf (mutawalli al-waqf) [cannot do so] because the custom in a long lease is to put a small amount of money in exchange for the first years and most of the money in exchange for the last year. So if the lease is for the orphan's land or waqf, the lease is not valid for the first years because it is less than the fair market rent (ujrat al-mithl), so it is not valid. And if he leases land for the orphan or waqf with waqf money, then in the last year the lease is at more than the fair market rent, so it is not valid. So if the lease is invalid in part in both cases, does it become valid in that which is beneficial for the orphan and waqf? According to the opinion that views the long lease as a single contract, no. According to the opinion that views it as multiple contracts, it is valid in what is beneficial, and not valid in what is harmful. The apparent view is invalidity in all. End quote. His statement: 'And it (i.e., the lease) should not exceed three years in awqaf so that the tenant does not claim ownership.' He said in al-Mukhtar: He [the author] expressed it generally, so it includes lands and others. And Sadr al-Shahid gave a fatwa that it should not exceed three years for lands and one year for others unless there is maslahah otherwise. He said in al-Muhit: The chosen view for fatwa is [the above]. End. And the meaning of the author when there is no condition from the waqf founder. If the founder specified something, and the trustee leases for more than that, it is not valid unless leasing it for more is more beneficial for the poor and people do not desire to rent it. Then the caretaker (qayyim) may raise the matter to the judge so that he may lease it for more, because the judge has authority over the poor and also over the deceased. The caretaker cannot lease it himself. This is in Fatawa Qadikhan. And by 'not valid' is meant lack of soundness (sihhah), meaning if the trustee leases waqf for more than three years, the lease is not valid. Sadr al-Shari'ah explicitly stated this, and it was said it is valid but then rescinded, mentioned by al-Shumni. And know that leasing waqf is not valid except at fair market rent or more. So if the trustee leases for less than fair market rent, the lease is not valid, and the tenant is obliged to pay the full fair market rent. In al-Khulasa there is an expression that gave the impression that the trustee is liable for the full fair market rent: 'The mutawalli of waqf leased for less than fair market rent, he is obliged to pay the full fair market rent.' End. Shaykh Qasim refuted this in his Fatawa, saying the pronoun refers to the lessee, as indicated in Talkhis al-Fatawa al-Kubra: '... as the author's statement: 'Nor should it be increased in awqaf to more than three years.' Al-Ramli said in al-Jawharah: And likewise the orphan's land. And the author of al-Bahr gave a fatwa equating the property of the orphan with waqf, as did his student Shaykh al-Allamah al-Ghazzi. Most of their discussion on the issue indicates that this is the chosen view and the mufta bihi, and the reason is that just as waqf is protected, so is the property [of the orphan].
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the rules for leasing waqf and orphan property. It begins by citing Fatawa Qadikhan concerning an executor leasing orphan land in a formal long lease (ijarah tawilah rasmiah) of three years. Such a lease is invalid because it typically involves small rent for early years and large rent for the last year, which results in unequal compensation. The discussion then focuses on whether a single contract that is partly invalid can be valid in the beneficial part. There are two opinions: one considers the long lease as one contract, so partial invalidity invalidates the whole; another considers it as separate contracts, so the beneficial part is valid. The apparent view is total invalidity. The text then turns to the matn statement that waqf leases should not exceed three years. This includes agricultural lands and other properties. Sadr al-Shahid differentiated: three years for lands, one year for others unless there is maslahah. The relied-upon fatwa is not to exceed three years. However, if the waqf founder specified a shorter term, the trustee cannot exceed it. If leasing for longer is more beneficial, the trustee must seek the judge's permission. Leasing at less than fair market rent is invalid, and the lessee must pay the full market rate. A subtle point: a phrase in al-Khulasa seemed to say the trustee bears the shortfall, but Shaykh Qasim clarified that it is the lessee who must pay the full market rate. Finally, the text extends the same three-year rule to orphan's property, equating it with waqf in terms of protection.

Key Terms
إجارة طويلة رسمية (ijarah tawilah rasmiah): A formal long-term lease where rent is concentrated in the final period, often with minimal rent in early years.
أجر المثل (ujrat al-mithl): Fair market rent for similar property.
وقف (waqf): Charitable endowment, corpus inalienable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the 'apparent view' (al-zahir) regarding a long lease with unequal rent for waqf property?
A) The lease is valid for the beneficial parts.
B) The lease is entirely invalid (fasad fi al-kull).
C) The lease is valid if the total rent equals market value.
D) The lease is valid only for the last year.

Key Takeaway
The apparent Hanafi view is that a long lease with unequal rent is entirely invalid.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease
intermediate
7 min
Rent Conditions and Extension to Orphan Property
Ruling on fair market rent condition and extension to orphan property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of fair market rent in waqf leases and identify who is liable for the shortfall.

Core Idea
Waqf leases must be at fair market rent; the lessee, not the trustee, pays the difference.

This lesson continues the discussion of waqf ijarah, focusing on the rent condition. The text states that leasing waqf below the fair market rent (ujrat al-mithl) invalidates the lease, and the lessee must pay the full market rate. A subtle point: an ambiguous phrase in al-Khulasa seemed to hold the trustee liable, but Shaykh Qasim clarified it is the lessee. Finally, the rule is extended to orphan property, treating it like waqf to protect vulnerable assets.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the requirement of fair market rent
Waqf leases are only valid if the rent is at least ujrat al-mithl (fair market rent). Leasing for less invalidates the contract.
This protects the waqf beneficiaries from undercompensation.
2
Analyse the ruling if lease is below market
The lease is invalid (fasid). The lessee is obliged to pay the full fair market rent (not just the agreed amount).
The contract is fasid, but the lessee's obligation is to pay the proper rent for the period of use.
3
Clarify the pronoun dispute
A phrase in al-Khulasa gave the impression that the mutawalli must pay the shortfall. Shaykh Qasim corrected this: the pronoun refers to the lessee (musta'jir).
The trustee does not personally guarantee the rent; the lessee bears the obligation.
4
Extend the rule to orphan property
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and others gave fatwa equating orphan property with waqf, applying the same conditions (fair rent, duration limits).
The rationale: orphan property, like waqf, must be protected from exploitation.
5
Note the exception for judicial authorization
If a longer lease or below-market rent is more beneficial (e.g., no tenants otherwise), the trustee must seek the judge's permission, not act unilaterally.
The judge has general authority over the poor and the deceased's estate.

Validity of Hire for Known Period and Future Contracts
Stating that a hire contract is valid for a known period even if the lessee's lifespan is shorter, and that future contracts are permissible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that makes a hire contract valid and explain the rulings on future contracts and the effect of sale.

Core Idea
A known period makes the benefit known — hire is valid even if the lessee may not survive the term.

This lesson examines a key ruling on hire (ijara) contracts from the Hanafi school. The passage clarifies that the validity of a hire contract hinges on the period being known, not on the lessee's expected lifespan. It also addresses the permissibility of future contracts (ijara mudafah) and the lessor's right to sell the property, which terminates the existing hire.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The passage states that a hire contract is valid (tajuz) if the period (muddah) is known (ma'lumah). The condition is that the period must be specified so that the benefit is known.
This is the default Hanafi position.
2
Examine the lifespan issue
Even if the period is longer than the lessee's probable lifespan, the contract remains valid because the benefit is considered known at the time of contract.
Al-Khassaf chose this view, though some scholars disagreed.
3
Analyze the future contract scenario
A future hire contract (e.g., 'I hire this house to you tomorrow') is permissible. The lessor retains ownership and can sell the property today.
The hire is contingent on the future start date, but the contract is binding upon agreement.
4
Consider the effect of sale
If the lessor sells the property before or during the hire period, the hire contract is rescinded (tantaqidu). The new owner is not bound by the hire.
This is stated in al-Khulasa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المدة إذا كانت معلومة كان قدر المنفعة فيها معلوماً فأفاد أنها تجوز ولو كانت المدة لا يعيش إلى مثلها عادة، واختاره الخصاف ومنعه بعضهم. وأفاد أنها تجوز مضافاً كما لو قال أجرتك هذه الدار غداً وللمؤجر بيعها اليوم وتنتقض الإجارة كما في الخلاصة.
If the period is known, the extent of the benefit in it is known. So it indicates that it is permissible even if the period is such that [the lessee] does not customarily live that long; al-Khassaf chose this, and some prohibited it. And it indicates that it is permissible as a future contract, like if one says 'I hire this house to you tomorrow,' and the lessor may sell it today, and the hire contract is rescinded, as in al-Khulasa.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This is a ruling on the validity of hire contracts. The passage clarifies that as long as the duration is known, the benefit is considered known, making the contract valid. It further notes that the contract can even be for a period beyond the probable lifespan of the lessee, as chosen by al-Khassaf, though some scholars disagree. Additionally, a future contract (e.g., 'I hire you tomorrow') is valid, but the lessor retains the right to sell the property today, which would terminate the hire.

Key Terms
المدة (al-muddah): The duration for which the hire contract is made; it must be known for validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition makes a hire contract valid?
A) The period must be exactly one year.
B) The period must be known.
C) The period must be less than the lessee's expected lifespan.
D) The contract must be immediate, not future.

Key Takeaway
A hire contract is valid if its period is known, even if the lessee may not survive the term; future contracts are allowed, but the lessor's sale rescinds the hire.

Guardian's Long-term Lease of Orphan's Land and Disguised Interest
Discussing the invalidity of a long-term lease of an orphan's land when rent is unevenly distributed to circumvent interest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on long-term leases of orphan's property with uneven rent, distinguish between the views treating it as one or multiple contracts, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Uneven rent in a long-term lease is a disguised riba device; the Hanafi view deems the entire contract invalid.

This passage from Fatawa Qadikhan addresses a common abuse: guardians back-loading rent in long-term leases to circumvent interest. The key issue is whether the lease is a single contract or a set of separate yearly contracts. We will trace the arguments and see why the preferred view declares total invalidity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal issue
What is the guardian doing? He signs a long-term lease (e.g., 3 years) with rent heavily weighted toward the final year. Why is this problematic?
Act: leasing orphan's or waqf land with a formal long lease.
2
Understand the rent structure
Rent for early years is below fair market rent (ujr al-mithl); rent for the last year is above. This disguises interest because the average may seem fair, but early termination would harm the orphan.
Key concept: ujr al-mithl must be satisfied for each rental period.
3
Examine the two intra-Hanafi views
One view treats the lease as a single contract ( عقد واحد ), making it entirely invalid. Another view treats it as multiple separate contracts (عقود), allowing partial validity for beneficial parts.
The passage names no specific scholars, but the dispute is within the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the reasoning to the scenario
Under the single-contract view, the entire lease is fasid (invalid). Under the multiple-contracts view, only the years where rent equals or exceeds ujr al-mithl might be valid.
The first years are invalid; the last year might be valid if it benefits the orphan.
5
Identify the tarjih (preferred view)
The author favors total invalidity (الظاهر هو الفساد في الكل) because the uniform practice shows it is one device to circumvent riba.
This is the mufta bihi position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : الوصي إذا أجر أرض اليتيم أو استأجر لليتيم أرضاً بمال اليتيم إجارة طويلة رسمية ثلاث سنين لا يجوز ذلك، وكذلك أبو الصغير ومتولي الوقف لأن الرسم في الإجارة الطويلة أن يجعل شيء يسير من مال الإجارة بمقابلة السنين الأول ومعظم المال بمقابلة السنة الأخيرة، فإن كانت الإجارة لأرض اليتيم أو الوقف لا تصح الإجارة في السنين الأول لأنها تكون بأقل من أجر المثل فلا تصح فإن استأجر أرضاً لليتيم أو الوقف بمال الوقف ففي السنة الأخيرة يكون الاستئجار بأكثر من أجر المثل فلا يصح، فإذا فسدت الإجارة في البعض في الوجهين هل يصح فيما كان خيراً لليتيم والوقف على قول من يجعل الإجارة الطويلة عقداً واحداً لا وعلى قول من يجعلها عقوداً يصح فيما كان خيراً لليتيم، ولا يصح فيما كان شراً له والظاهر هو الفساد في الكل ا هـ.
In Fatawa Qadikhan: If a guardian leases out an orphan's land or leases land for an orphan using the orphan's wealth with a long formal lease of three years, it is not permissible. Similarly for the guardian of a minor and the caretaker of a waqf. Because the practice in long-term leases is to assign a small amount of the rent in exchange for the first years and most of the money in exchange for the last year. If the lease is for an orphan's land or waqf, the lease is not valid for the first years because they are for less than the fair rent, so it is not valid. And if he leases land for an orphan or waqf using waqf funds, then in the last year the lease would be for more than the fair rent, so it is not valid. If the lease is invalid in part, in both cases, is it valid in what is beneficial for the orphan and waqf? According to the one who considers the long lease as a single contract: no. According to the one who considers it as multiple contracts: it is valid in what is beneficial for the orphan, and not valid in what is harmful. The apparent view is that it is entirely invalid.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage discusses a critical issue: guardians often use long-term leases to circumvent riba by allocating very low rent for early years and high rent for the final year, making the average fair but the individual years unfair. This is not valid because the orphan or waqf suffers loss if the lease is terminated early. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the lease is considered one contract, it is entirely invalid; if considered multiple contracts, the parts that benefit the orphan might be valid. The preferred view is total invalidity.

Key Terms
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): Fair market rent; the rent that similar properties command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a long-term lease of orphan's land with uneven payments invalid?
A) Because the total rent is too high.
B) Because the lease exceeds three years.
C) Because the first years are below market rent and the last year is above, harming the orphan.
D) Because the guardian lacks authority.

Key Takeaway
A long-term lease with unevenly distributed rent where early years are below fair market rent is invalid in its entirety to prevent disguised riba.

The Three-Year Limit for Waqf Leases
Stating the primary rule on waqf lease duration and rent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default maximum term for leasing waqf property and identify the requirement of fair market rent.

Core Idea
Waqf leases default to three years to prevent ownership claims; leasing must be at or above fair market rent.

This passage establishes a key protective measure for waqf properties: they cannot be leased for more than three years without special permission. This prevents the lessee from claiming ownership through prolonged possession. Additionally, any lease must be at or above the fair market rent (ujr al-mithl) to safeguard the beneficiaries' interests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Find the explicit statement that waqf property should not be leased for more than three years. This is the default rule.
The text says: 'ولا تزاد في الأوقاف على ثلاث سنين'.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason given is to prevent the lessee from claiming ownership. Consider how long possession can lead to such claims.
The word 'كيلا يدعي المستأجر ملكها' indicates the legal purpose.
3
Note the fair market rent condition
The passage states that leasing waqf is only permissible at or above ujr al-mithl. Any lease below this is invalid, and the lessee must pay the full market rent.
The text: 'إجارة الوقف لا تجوز إلا بأجرة المثل أو أكثر'.
4
Recognize the consequence of violation
If the lease exceeds three years without valid reason, it is invalid (or some say valid but rescinded). The mufta bihi is that it is invalid.
The author clarifies: 'المراد بعدم الجواز عدم الصحة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا تزاد في الأوقاف على ثلاث سنين كيلا يدعي المستأجر ملكها. قال في المختار . أطلقه فشمل الضياع وغيره. وقد أفتى الصدر الشهيد بعدم الزيادة على ثلاث سنين في الضياع وعلى سنة في غيرها إلا إذا كانت المصلحة في غيره. قال في المحيط : المختار للفتوى ا.هـ. ومراد المصنف عند عدم شرط الواقف فإن نص على شيء فاجره الناظر أكثر منه لا يجوز إلا إذا كانت إجارتها أكثر أنفع للفقراء والناس لا يرغبون في استئجارها فللقيم أن يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي حتى يؤاجرها أكثر لأن للقاضي ولاية النظر على الفقراء وعلى الميت أيضاً، وليس للقيم أن يؤاجرها بنفسه ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان. والمراد بعدم الجواز عدم الصحة يعني لو أجر الناظر الوقف أكثر من ثلاث سنين لا تصح الإجارة صرح به صدر الشريعة، وقيل تصح وتنفسخ ذكره الشمني واعلم أن إجارة الوقف لا تجوز إلا بأجرة المثل أو أكثر فلو أجر الناظر بدون أجرة المثل لا تصح الإجارة ويلزم المستأجر تمام أجر المثل، وقد وقع في الخلاصة عبارة أوهمت أن الناظر يضمن تمام أجر المثل فقال : متولي الوقف أجر بدون أجر المثل يلزمه تمام أجر المثل ا هـ . وقد رده الشيخ قاسم في فتاواه بأن الضمير يرجع إلى المستأجر يدل عليه ما ذكره في تلخيص الفتاوى الكبرى وعبارته : كما قول المصنف : (ولا) تزاد في الأوقاف على ثلاث سنين قال الرملي وفي الجوهرة: وعلى هذا أرض اليتيم وقد أفتى صاحب البحر بإلحاق عقار اليتيم بالوقف وكذا تلميذه الشيخ العلامة الغزي، وأكثر كلامهم في المسألة يدل على أنه المختار وأنه المفتى به وعلته أنه كما يصان الوقف يصان مال اليتيم عن دعوى الملك بطول المدة بل مال اليتيم أولى للنصوص الموجبة له المصرحة بالنهي عن قربانه فليكن عليه المعوّل وأقول أيضاً: ومثل عقار اليتيم عقار بيت المال فتأمل.
His statement: 'And it should not be increased in endowments beyond three years, so that the lessee does not claim ownership.' He said in al-Mukhtar: He made it absolute, so it includes lands and others. And al-Sadr al-Shahid gave a fatwa not to increase beyond three years for lands and one year for others, unless there is a benefit otherwise. He said in al-Muhit: The chosen view for fatwa. End. And the author's intent is when the waqf founder stipulated nothing. If he stipulated something, the overseer may not lease for more unless leasing for more is more beneficial for the poor and people are not inclined to rent it. Then the custodian may raise the matter to the judge so he can lease it for more, because the judge has authority over the poor and over the deceased. The custodian may not lease it himself; thus in Fatawa Qadikhan. And the intent of 'not permissible' is 'not valid', meaning if the overseer leases the waqf for more than three years, the lease is not valid, declared by Sadr al-Shari'a. And it is said: it is valid but then rescinded, mentioned by al-Shumnni. And know that leasing waqf is not permissible except for fair market rent or more. So if the overseer leases for less than market rent, the lease is not valid, and the lessee is obliged to pay the full market rent. And a phrase occurred in al-Khulasa that suggested the overseer guarantees the full market rent: 'The custodian of the waqf leased for less than market rent, he must pay the full market rent.' But Shaykh Qasim in his fatawa refuted this, saying the pronoun refers to the lessee, as indicated by what he mentioned in Talkhis al-Fatawa al-Kubra, and his phrase: as the author's statement: 'And it should not be increased...' al-Ramli said in al-Jawhara: 'And likewise for the orphan's land.' And the author of al-Bahr gave a fatwa equating the orphan's real estate with waqf, as did his student al-Shaykh al-Allama al-Ghazzi. Most of their discussions indicate that this is the chosen view and the one given fatwa. The reason is that just as waqf is protected, so is the orphan's wealth from claims of ownership by long duration; rather, the orphan's wealth is more deserving due to the texts that obligate it, clearly prohibiting approaching it. So let this be relied upon. And I say: Likewise for the public treasury's real estate. Consider.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage addresses the maximum duration for leasing waqf property. The general ruling is that it should not exceed three years to prevent the lessee from claiming ownership through long possession. However, there are exceptions: if the waqf founder specified a different term, or if leasing for longer is more beneficial for the beneficiaries, the custodian must seek court approval. The passage also clarifies that even if the lease is valid in duration, it must be at market rent or more; otherwise it is invalid and the lessee must pay full market rent. There is a clarification about who is liable — the lessee, not the custodian.

Key Terms
الوقف (al-waqf): An endowment of property in perpetuity for charitable purposes.
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): Fair market rent for similar property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of leasing a waqf property for five years without court approval?
A) The lease is valid for three years, then invalid.
B) The lease is invalid from the start.
C) The lease is valid if the rent is above market.
D) The lease is valid but the custodian must compensate.

Key Takeaway
The default rule for waqf leases is a maximum of three years and at fair market rent; violations render the lease invalid.

Exceptions and Extensions: Waqf Lease Beyond Three Years
Stating exceptions and extensions to the three-year waqf lease rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exceptions to the three-year limit for waqf leases and apply the analogy to orphan's and public treasury property.

Core Idea
The three-year limit can be overridden by a founder's stipulation or court approval, and the same protection extends to orphan's and public treasury property.

While the default rule prohibits waqf leases beyond three years, the passage provides important exceptions and extensions. The waqf founder may set a different term, and the custodian can seek court approval if longer is more beneficial. Furthermore, the ruling is extended by analogy to orphan's real estate and public treasury property, emphasizing the broader principle of protecting vulnerable assets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exceptions to the three-year rule
Locate where the passage allows a longer term. There are two main exceptions: when the waqf founder stipulates a term, and when it is more beneficial and approved by a judge.
The text says: 'مراد المصنف عند عدم شرط الواقف' and 'فللقيم أن يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي'.
2
Understand the court approval process
The custodian cannot unilaterally exceed three years; he must go to the judge. The judge has authority over the beneficiaries and can authorize a longer lease.
The phrase 'لأن للقاضي ولاية النظر على الفقراء وعلى الميت أيضاً' explains the judge's role.
3
Examine the analogy to orphan's property
The passage equates orphan's real estate with waqf for the same purpose: preventing claims of ownership through long possession. Note the reasoning: 'كما يصان الوقف يصان مال اليتيم'.
Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq extended this ruling to orphan's property, and the author supports it.
4
Consider the extension to public treasury property
The author adds that the same rule applies to public treasury (bayt al-mal) real estate, indicating a general principle of protecting vulnerable assets.
The text ends with 'ومثل عقار اليتيم عقار بيت المال فتأمل'.

Effect of Market Rent Changes on Waqf Leases
Explaining that a lease is not invalidated by a drop in rent, but may be invalidated by a general increase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on rescission of a waqf lease due to market rent changes and apply it to scenarios of increase and decrease.

Core Idea
A waqf lease is not invalidated by a drop in rent, but a universal market increase may rescind the contract.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the effect of market rent fluctuations on a waqf lease. It distinguishes between a decrease and an increase, and between a universal increase and a spiteful increase. Understanding this ruling is crucial for managing waqf properties fairly, ensuring the waqf benefits without unjustly harming the lessee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling for stable or decreased rent
Note that if the market rent remains unchanged or decreases during the lease term, the original contract remains valid and is not rescinded. The lessee agreed to the terms, and a drop does not harm the waqf.
The lessee's consent at the outset binds them to the fixed rent.
2
Recognize when a universal increase triggers rescission
If market rent rises universally (not just in one isolated instance), the contract may be rescinded and a new contract made at the higher rate. This protects the waqf from loss of income.
The increase must be general ('inda al-kull), not a single spiteful offer.
3
Apply the exception for properties that cannot be vacated
If the property has unharvested crops or similar, rescission is not possible immediately. Instead, the named rent is due proportionally up to the increase, and the market rent after.
The lessee is not forced to breach the contract; the rent adjusts for the remaining period.
4
Distinguish a spiteful increase from a universal one
A single person offering a higher rent out of spite does not invalidate the contract. The increase must be a general market trend.
This prevents manipulation and protects the lessee's rights.
5
Generalize to all waqf properties
The same rule applies to shops, mills, and any waqf asset leased from the custodian (mutawalli). The goal is to benefit the waqf without causing injustice.
This is the preferred view according to Qadikhan and al-Allama Qasim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الأسبيجابي في المزارعة إذا كانت الأرض أرض وقف استأجرها من المتولي إلى طويل المدة ينظر ؛ إن كان السعر بحاله لم يزدد ولم ينقص كما كان وقت العقد فإنه يجوز . وإن غلا أجر مثلها فإنه يفسخ ذلك العقد ويحتاج إلى تجديد ذلك العقد ثانياً، وكذلك إذا استأجرها بأجرة معلومة إلى سنة فلما مضى نصف السنة غلا سعرها وازداد أجر مثلها فإنه يفسخ ذلك العقد ويعقد ثانياً على أجرة معلومة ولو كانت الأرض بحال لم يمكن فسخها نحو ما إذا كان فيها زرع لم يحصد بعد ولم يدرك بعد فلا يمكن فسخها ولكن إلى وقت زيادته يجب المسمى بقدره وبعد الزيادة إلى تمام السنة يجب أجر مثلها. وأما إذا كان ينتقص من أجرتها يعني رخص أجرتها وسعرها قبل مضي المدة فإن الإجارة لا تبطل ولا لأن المستأجر قد رضي بذلك حيث عقد عليها وزيادة الأجرة إنما تعتبر إذا زادت عند الكل، فأما إذا زاد واحد في أجرتها تعنتاً على المستأجر الأول فلا يعتبر ذلك ولا يبطل العقد ولا يفسخ ما لم تمض المدة، وكذلك حكم الحانوت والطاحونة وجميع ما يكون وقفاً استؤجر من المتولي ا.هـ. وكذا ذكر قاضيخان في فتاواه ورجحه العلامة قاسم في فتاواه بأنه أنفع للوقف
And al-Asbijabi mentioned in al-Muzara'a: If the land is waqf land and someone leases it from the custodian for a long period, it is considered: if the price remains as it was, neither increased nor decreased as at the time of contract, then it is permissible. If its rent increases (market rent rises), then the contract is rescinded and needs to be renewed a second time. Similarly, if he leased it for a fixed rent for one year, and after half the year its rent increases and market rent rises, then the contract is rescinded and a new contract is made at a fixed rent. If the land cannot be rescinded (e.g., crops not yet harvested), it cannot be rescinded, but until the increase, the named rent is due proportionally, and after the increase until the end of the year, the market rent is due. However, if the rent decreases (i.e., goes down) before the term ends, the lease is not invalidated because the lessee agreed to that when contracting. And an increase in rent is only considered if it increases universally. As for if one person increases the rent out of spite against the first lessee, that is not considered, and the contract is not invalidated or rescinded until the term ends. The same applies to shops, mills, and all that is waqf leased from the custodian. End. Similarly, Qadikhan mentioned in his fatawa, and al-Allama Qasim preferred it in his fatawa, because it is more beneficial for the waqf.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of changes in market rent on a waqf lease. The general principle is that a lease remains valid despite a drop in rent, as the lessee has agreed to the terms. However, if market rent rises generally, the lease may be rescinded and a new contract made at the higher rate. If the property cannot be vacated (e.g., crops are still growing), the named rent is due up to the increase and market rent thereafter. The ruling aims to protect the waqf from loss, but not to penalize the lessee for random increases. This is the relied-upon view.

Key Terms
السعر (al-si'r): The market price or rent for similar property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is a waqf lease rescinded due to rent change?
A) If the rent decreases.
B) If a single person raises the rent out of spite.
C) If the market rent increases universally.
D) If the lessee fails to pay.

Key Takeaway
A waqf lease remains valid despite a drop in rent, but a universal market increase rescinds it — unless the property cannot be vacated, in which case rent adjusts proportionally.
