Two Definitions of 'Athar: Connected Trace vs. Visible Effect
Two definitions of 'athar and their effect on the right of detention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two Hanafi definitions of 'athar and apply each to determine whether a worker has the right to detain.

Core Idea
A worker's right to detain depends on whether his 'athar is an attached trace or merely a visible effect — two definitions, two outcomes.

In Hanafi law, a hired worker may retain possession of the object of work until his wage is paid, but only if his work leaves an 'athar (trace) on the object. But what exactly counts as a trace? The school's commentators disagree: does it require an attached effect like sawdust or dye, or is any observable change enough? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents both views and their practical implications for workers like woodcutters, millers, and barbers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the passage and extract the two definitions of 'athar: (1) an effect connected to the locus of work (muttasil), like sawdust and dye; (2) something seen and observed in the locus of work, even if not attached.
These definitions lead to different outcomes for detention.
2
Apply the first definition
Under the first definition, only workers whose work leaves a physically attached effect (dyer, fuller) can detain. Woodcutters, millers, and barbers cannot because the effect is not attached after the work is complete.
The reasoning is that the contracted benefit is an attribute in the object.
3
Apply the second definition
Under the second definition, any worker whose work produces a visible change (e.g., broken wood, ground grain, shaved head) has a right to detain, even if no material is attached.
This view is supported by al-Qunyah.
4
Note the author's lean and exception
The author appears to prefer the first definition based on the ta'lil (reason) given in al-Hidayah, but acknowledges the second view. Also note the exception: a retriever of a runaway slave can detain even though his work leaves no trace, because the slave was saved from perishing.
This exception shows that other factors can override the 'athar requirement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في المراد من الأثر فقيل أن تكون الأثرة متصلة بمحل العمل كالنشار ،والصبغ وقيل أن يرى ويعاين في محل العمل، وثمرته تظهر في كسر الحطب وطحن الحنطة وحلق رأس العبد فليس له الحبس على الأول، وله الحبس على الثاني. وظاهر ما في القنية ترجيح الثاني، والذي يظهر من كلامهم ترجيح الأول لما عللوا به في حق الحبس من أن المعقود عليه وصف في الثوب ومنهم من ضبط الحمال بالحاء المهملة، ومنهم من ضبطها بالجيم والأولى الأول لأن الحمل يجوز أن يقع على الظهر وعلى الدابة فيكون أعم من لفظ الجمال بالجيم. ولا يرد الآبق حيث يكون للراد حق حبسه لاستيفاء الجعل ولا أثر لعمله لأنه كان على شرف الهلاك وقد أحياه فكأنه باعه منه فله حق الحبس ؛ كذا في الهداية .
And they disagreed about the meaning of 'athar (trace). It was said: it should be an effect connected to the locus of work, like sawdust and dye. And it was said: it is that which is seen and observed in the locus of work, and its fruit appears in breaking firewood, grinding wheat, and shaving a slave's head. So on the first view, he has no right of detention; on the second, he has the right of detention. And the apparent in al-Qunyah is preference for the second. And what appears from their statements is preference for the first, because of what they gave as reason in the context of detention: that the contracted object is an attribute in the cloth. And some of them write 'hammal' with the unpointed ha', and some write it with the jim; the first is better because 'haml' can occur on the back and on the mount, so it is more general than the word 'jammal' with jim. And the return of a runaway slave is not included, because the returnee has the right to detain for the reward (ju'l), even though his work has no trace, because the slave was on the verge of perishing and he revived him, as if he sold him to him, so he has the right of detention; thus in al-Hidaya.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage is a definitional discussion within the Hanafi school about the precise meaning of 'athar (trace) that grants a worker the right of detention. There are two opinions. The first opinion (qil) defines 'athar as an effect that is physically attached to the object (muttasil bi-mahall al-'amal), such as sawdust from sawing or dye from dyeing. According to this, a worker who breaks firewood, grinds grain, or shaves a slave's head does not have a trace because the effect is not attached to the object after the work. Therefore, such a worker cannot detain the object for his wage. The second opinion (qil) defines 'athar as something that can be seen and observed (yura wa yu'ayan) in the locus of work, even if not physically attached. Under this definition, the same workers (woodcutter, miller, barber) do have a trace because the effect of their work is visible (e.g., the wood is broken, the grain is ground). Consequently, they have the right of detention. The author mentions that the text of al-Qunyah prefers the second opinion, but the apparent view from other scholars (and the wording of al-Hidayah) suggests the first opinion is preferred because the reasoning for detention is that the contracted object is an attribute in the cloth (i.e., an attached attribute). The passage also clarifies the word 'hammal' (porter) – some spell it with ha (حمال) and some with jim (جمال meaning camel driver). The author prefers the first because 'haml' (carrying) can be on the back or on an animal, making it more general. Finally, it distinguishes the case of returning a runaway slave: even though his work (capture) leaves no trace, the returnee has the right to detain the slave for the promised reward because the slave was on the verge of perishing and the returnee revived him, as if he bought him. This exception is from al-Hidaya.

Key Terms
أثر (athar): In this context, the effect of work on an object; two definitions: attached effect (sawdust, dye) or observable effect (broken wood, ground grain).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion (the one preferred by the author's cited scholars), does a woodcutter who splits firewood have the right to detain the wood for his wage?
A) Yes, because the effect (splitting) is visible
B) No, because the effect is not attached to the wood after splitting
C) Yes, by analogy with the dyer
D) No, because woodcutting is not a service contract

Key Takeaway
The right of a worker to detain the object of hire depends on which definition of 'athar is adopted; the preferred view is that the trace must be attached to the object, though the second view is also transmitted.

Partial Performance in Hire Contracts: Proportional Wage for Bringing Family Members
Ruling on wage when some family members die before delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wage entitlement for partial delivery in a hire contract and differentiate between known and unknown subjects.

Core Idea
Partial fulfillment of a hire contract entitles to proportional wage.

Hire contracts sometimes involve bringing multiple persons or items for a single lump sum. If some die or are lost en route, does the hired person receive any wage? The Hanafi school answers with a clear principle: the wage is proportionally due for what was delivered, provided the subjects were individually known. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its rationale, drawing from al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contract and Event
The contract is a hire (ijara) to bring family members. Some die before delivery; the hired person brings the survivors. The question: what wage is due?
The family members are the 'ma'qud 'alayh' (contracted subject).
2
Apply the Principle: Awfa Ba'd al-Ma'qud 'Alayh
The hired person fulfilled part of the contracted obligation. In Hanafi law, this entitles to a proportional wage (ajr bi-hisabihi). The rationale: the wage is consideration for the service of bringing each individual, so partial performance gives partial wage.
This is not a void contract; partial performance is compensable.
3
Consider the Condition: Known vs Unknown Subjects
If the family members were known (e.g., named individuals), the wage can be apportioned per head. If unknown (e.g., just 'bring my family'), the entire wage is due because the contract is for the service of bringing whoever belongs to the family, and the hired person has done that.
The condition is crucial: known subjects allow proportional reduction; unknown subjects mean full wage is owed.
4
Apply to Similar Cases
This principle extends to hiring a porter to carry specified goods: if some goods are destroyed en route, he gets paid for what he delivered. But if the goods were unspecified, full wage is due as long as he brought something.
Each case depends on whether the contracted subjects were individually identifiable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن استأجره ليجيء بعياله فمات بعضهم فجاء بما بقي فله أجره بحسابه لأنه أو فى بعض المعقود عليه فيستحق الأجر بقدره ومراده إذا كانوا معلومين ليكون الأجر مقابلاً بجملتهم، وإن كانوا غير معلومين يجب الأجر كله إليه أشار في الهداية
His saying: (And if he hired him to bring his family, and some of them died, and he came with those who remained, then he has his wage proportionately) because he fulfilled part of the contracted object, so he deserves wage in proportion. And his intent is when they are known (known individuals) so that the wage is in consideration of the whole. If they are unknown, the entire wage is due to him; al-Hidaya indicated this.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This is a ruling on partial performance in a hire contract for bringing persons. If a person hires someone to bring his family members (e.g., from a journey) for a lump sum, and some of them die during the journey, the hired person is entitled to a proportional wage for the number of survivors he delivers. The rationale is that he fulfilled part of what was contracted (ba'd al-ma'qud 'alayh). The condition is that the family members were known (ma'lumin) individually, so the wage can be apportioned. If they were unknown (e.g., only a number but unspecified individuals), then he is entitled to the full wage because it is as if the contract was for the service of bringing any family members, and he brought whoever remained. The passage references al-Hidaya for this elaboration.

Key Terms
أوفى بعض المعقود عليه (awfa ba'd al-ma'qud 'alayh): Fulfilled part of the contracted obligation; in hire contracts, this entitles to a proportional wage.
المعقود عليه (al-ma'qud 'alayh): The object or subject of the contract; what the contract is about. In this passage, it refers to the family members to be brought.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man hires a carrier to bring his three sons from another city for 30 dirhams. One son dies en route, and the carrier brings the remaining two. How much wage does he receive?
A) 30 dirhams
B) 20 dirhams
C) 10 dirhams
D) Nothing, because the contract was for all three

Key Takeaway
Partial performance of a divisible hire contract entitles the hired party to a proportional wage under Hanafi law, with the condition that the subjects must be individually known for proportional reduction to apply.

Rulings on the Sailor's Wage and Substitution
Commentary on matn rulings about the sailor and substitution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a sailor cannot be imprisoned for his wage, and explain when he may substitute another worker.

Core Idea
A hired sailor cannot be imprisoned for unpaid wages; but if his contract is unspecified, he may hire a substitute.

This lesson examines a key ruling from the Book of Ijarah: the prohibition on imprisoning a sailor for his wage. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains both the ruling and its implications for substitution. Understanding these rules clarifies the limits of the hirer's rights and the worker's flexibility under Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
The text states: 'And the sailor is not imprisoned for the wage.' This is a prohibition on the hirer, meaning he cannot detain the sailor's person or property to extract payment.
This applies to all hired workers (ajir), not just sailors.
2
Examine the condition for substitution
If the contract stipulates that the sailor must do the work himself, he cannot employ another. But if it is absolute (unstated), he may hire a substitute because the duty is a liability in his debt, similar to paying a debt.
The substitution is a form of performing a financial obligation.
3
Analyze the proportional wage scenario
If hired to bring his family, and some die en route, he receives a proportional wage for those he actually brought. This follows the principle that payment is for work actually done.
Proportion is based on the number of persons or effort expended.
4
Note the carrier rulings
No wage is due for carrying a book for a response (expecting a reply) or carrying food that is returned due to death. The work is incomplete or wasted.
The ruling prevents payment for fruitless labor.
5
Understand the scope of 'mallaah'
The term 'mallaah' (sailor) is here used broadly to include any worker hired for transport, including camel drivers (jammal).
The commentary clarifies that the word is more general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإجارة
والملاح لا يحبس للأجر ولا يستعمل غيره إن شرط عمله بنفسه وإن أطلق فله أن يستأجر غيره وإن استأجره ليجيء بعياله فمات بعضهم فجاء بمن بقي فله أجره بحسابه ولا أجر الحامل الكتاب للجواب أو الحامل الطعام إن رده للموت.
وعلى الدابة فيكون أعم من لفظ الجمال بالجيم. ولا يرد الآبق حيث يكون للراد حق حبسه لاستيفاء الجعل ولا أثر لعمله لأنه كان على شرف الهلاك وقد أحياه فكأنه باعه منه فله حق الحبس؛ كذا في الهداية.
Book of Ijarah. The sailor is not imprisoned for the wage, nor does he use another if he stipulated his own work; but if he gave absolute permission, he may hire another. If he hired him to bring his family, and some died, and he brought the remaining, he has his wage in proportion. No wage for the carrier of a book for a response or the carrier of food if he returns it due to death. And upon the beast, so it is more general than the word 'jammal' with the letter jim. And the runaway slave is not returned, where the finder has the right to detain him to claim the reward, and his work has no effect because he was on the verge of destruction and he revived him, so it is as if he sold him to him, so he has the right of detention; thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by stating that a sailor (ملاح) who is hired cannot be imprisoned by the hirer for his wage. The ruling then addresses substitution: if the contract stipulates that the sailor must do the work himself, he cannot employ another; but if it is unspecified (مطلق), he may hire a substitute because the duty is a liability in his debt, similar to paying a debt. Next, if someone is hired to bring his family members, and some die en route, he receives a proportional wage for those brought. Finally, if someone carries a book for a response or food and returns it due to death, no wage is due. The commentary clarifies that the term 'ملاح' is general, covering both sailors and camel drivers. Then it discusses the runaway slave: the finder (راد) is not required to return him; he may detain him until he receives the reward (جعل) because his act of capturing saved the slave from near destruction. This is analogous to a sale—he effectively purchased the slave from the owner, hence he has the right of retention. This ruling is from al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
ملاح (mallaah): A sailor or boatman, also used broadly for any worker hired for transport.
أجر (ajr): Wage or hire payment.
استئجار (isti'jar): Hiring someone else to perform the work.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition may a sailor hire another to do the work?
A) Only if the hirer agrees.
B) Only if the contract stipulates his own work.
C) If the contract is absolute (no stipulation).
D) He may never hire another.

Key Takeaway
A hired sailor cannot be imprisoned for his wage; substitution is allowed when the contract does not specify personal work.

Rulings on the Runaway Slave's Detention by the Finder
Commentary on matn ruling about the runaway slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that the finder of a runaway slave may detain him for reward, and articulate the qiyas reasoning that likens this to a sale.

Core Idea
The finder of a runaway slave may detain him to claim the reward, just as a seller retains goods until paid.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the passage: the ruling on the runaway slave (الآبق). The commentary explains that the finder (راد) has the right to detain the slave until he receives the reward (جعل). This is justified through qiyas (analogy) to a sale, because the finder's act of capturing saves the slave from destruction, effectively purchasing him from the owner. Understanding this qiyas illuminates Hanafi reasoning on similar cases.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on the runaway slave
The text says: 'ولا يرد الآبق' meaning the finder is not required to return the slave immediately; he may detain him.
This is not a prohibition on returning; it is a right to detain.
2
Identify the basis: the reward (ju'l)
The finder has the right to detain 'لاستيفاء الجعل' i.e., to claim the reward promised by the owner.
The reward is a form of commission for finding and returning.
3
Explain the qiyas to sale
The commentary says: 'فكأنه باعه منه' - it is as if the finder bought the slave from the owner. Because the slave was on the verge of destruction and the finder 'revived' him by capturing.
The analogy is based on saving from destruction (ihya).
4
Understand the illa (effective cause)
The illa is that the finder's act prevented total loss, similar to a buyer who pays for an item. Hence, he has a right to retain until paid.
The qiyas is jaliyyah (clear analogy).
5
Note the source authority
The ruling is attributed to al-Hidayah, indicating it is from the relied-upon positions (Zahir al-Riwayah).
Commentary says 'كذا في الهداية'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإجارة
والملاح لا يحبس للأجر ولا يستعمل غيره إن شرط عمله بنفسه وإن أطلق فله أن يستأجر غيره وإن استأجره ليجيء بعياله فمات بعضهم فجاء بمن بقي فله أجره بحسابه ولا أجر الحامل الكتاب للجواب أو الحامل الطعام إن رده للموت.
وعلى الدابة فيكون أعم من لفظ الجمال بالجيم. ولا يرد الآبق حيث يكون للراد حق حبسه لاستيفاء الجعل ولا أثر لعمله لأنه كان على شرف الهلاك وقد أحياه فكأنه باعه منه فله حق الحبس؛ كذا في الهداية.
Book of Ijarah. The sailor is not imprisoned for the wage, nor does he use another if he stipulated his own work; but if he gave absolute permission, he may hire another. If he hired him to bring his family, and some died, and he brought the remaining, he has his wage in proportion. No wage for the carrier of a book for a response or the carrier of food if he returns it due to death. And upon the beast, so it is more general than the word 'jammal' with the letter jim. And the runaway slave is not returned, where the finder has the right to detain him to claim the reward, and his work has no effect because he was on the verge of destruction and he revived him, so it is as if he sold him to him, so he has the right of detention; thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by stating that a sailor (ملاح) who is hired cannot be imprisoned by the hirer for his wage. The ruling then addresses substitution: if the contract stipulates that the sailor must do the work himself, he cannot employ another; but if it is unspecified (مطلق), he may hire a substitute because the duty is a liability in his debt, similar to paying a debt. Next, if someone is hired to bring his family members, and some die en route, he receives a proportional wage for those brought. Finally, if someone carries a book for a response or food and returns it due to death, no wage is due. The commentary clarifies that the term 'ملاح' is general, covering both sailors and camel drivers. Then it discusses the runaway slave: the finder (راد) is not required to return him; he may detain him until he receives the reward (جعل) because his act of capturing saved the slave from near destruction. This is analogous to a sale—he effectively purchased the slave from the owner, hence he has the right of retention. This ruling is from al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
آبق (aabiq): A runaway slave who has fled from his master.
جعل (ju‘l): A reward or commission given to the finder for returning a runaway slave.
رد (radd): Return of the runaway slave to the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the finder of a runaway slave have the right to detain him?
A) Because he is the new owner.
B) Because the owner has abandoned the slave.
C) Because he saved the slave from destruction, analogous to a sale.
D) Because the slave consented.

Key Takeaway
The finder of a runaway slave may detain him until the reward is paid, justified by qiyas to a sale because his capture saved the slave from destruction.
